Abstract
The project was proposed to study the hypercomplex structure of solid mechanics, ex-
pressing related variables and their relations in terms of the language of Clifford algebra or

Supplement 2 
Qualitative analysis and classification of an elastoplastic model with 
nonlinear kinematic-isotropic hardening-softening 
Hong-Ki Hong and Li-Wei Liu 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In this model set, models which share similar characteristics can be put together to form a subset,
so the model set is separated into several subsets. For example, the pressure sensitive models form
a subset denoted Mp and the pressure insensitive models form another subset denoted Mnp. If
the subset of the pressure sensitive models can be partitioned further into, say, two subsetsM1p and
M2p, which exhibit different characteristic modes of behavior, the model setM are thus categorized
as follows.
M
ï£±ï£´ï£²ï£´ï£³ Mp
{
M1p
M2p
Mnp
Nonetheless, there arises the question how to classify subsets and identify a material under in-
vestigation to be in an appropriate subset when no prior information about its categorization is
available?
To answer this crucial question, let us point out that it is tacitly assumed that a parameterization
of all models in the model setM(Ïƒ, ,Î±,Î¸) be furnished. This assumption enables us to reduce
the problem of classifying models to that of classifying parameters. Suppose the number of real-
valued parameters is r, then there are at most 2r subsets of models in the model setM according
as whether each and every parameter is zero or nonzero. However, such a kind of categorization of
2r subsets does not really reveal the underlining internal structure ofM. With the parametrization,
it is sometimes more convenient to deem the model setM as a (mega) modelM, and as such, to
deem subsets of models in the model set, subsets of subsets, and so on, all as submodels of the
(mega) moelM. With respect to the (mega) modelM, one common practice is to estimate all the
parameters of M indiscriminately and reset the values of those parameters whose estimated val-
ues are very close to zero, to be zeros. Then according to the reset zero-value parameters and the
estimated values of the remaining parameters, one can decide which submodel the investigated ma-
terial belongs to. However, this indiscriminate all-together estimation algorithm often suffers from
numerical instability and local trapping when its solution strategy has more than one solution basin,
for the (mega) model M consists of more than one characteristic mode of behavior. To circum-
vent these drawbacks one had better perform a qualitative analysis before a quantitative estimation
such that the numerical effort can be directed solely to a single subset of more uniform constitutive
hehavior, which has been distinguished from the other subsets in the qualitative analysis, rather
than to the whole model set.
In this paper we explore an aspect of the underlining internal structure of elastoplasticity,
namely the evolution rules governing plastic flow and hardening-softening. To best describe the
evolution, from the plasic strain p and backstress Ïƒb distinguished is the intrinsic time Î» among
2
tection, the estimation of yield function parameters, the estimation of backstress, the recognition of
submodel, and the estimation of parameters within the submodel in sequence. What the methods
of parameter estimation should be used? How to estimate the parameters from experimental data?
These subjects are beyond the scope of this article.
The organization of the paper is as follows. Section 2 introduces a 9+Niso parameters model of
elastoplasticity which plays the role of the model set. Section 3 converts the model to an equivalent
vector form. Further converting it to an equivalent state-space form, Section 4 perform qualita-
tive analysis. Section 5 is devoted to classification resulted from the qualitative analysis, finding
criteria for submodels and exploring their characteristics. Section 6 oulines a combined-qualitative-
quantitative identification procedure based on the classification. Conclusions are drawn in Section
7.

The above state-space form (3) is suitable for the ensuing qualitative analysis.
Now let us proceed to perform qualitative analysis of the internal state x of the 9+Niso parame-
ters model in the form (3). We must search to find a variety of suitable linear combinations of (3),
that is, to pre-multiply the internal state evolution equation (3)2 by appropriate constant matrices of
linear combinations on the left. In this way we shall obtain a variety of submodels.
To secure all the so called appropriate constant matrices of linear combinations, we transform
Eq.(3)2 into principal directions and then construct a 12Ã— 12 matrix
L :=

where the first s columns are the independent columns of the matrix L and the remaining 12 âˆ’ s
columns are so picked as to ensure the matrix Î¦ nonsingular. The last 12âˆ’s rows are to be collected
as a linear combination matrix which when taken to pre-multiply the transformed equation on the
left results in a submodel. The linear combination matrices of all submodels, which will be further
examined one by one in the next section, are listed in Table 2.
The submodels belongs to two categories, one denoted A and the other F . Category A is a
collection of submodels Amn which exhibit specific behavior ofAutonomic evolution. In these sub-
models, there exist m combinations of the plastic strain qp and the backstress Qb which are not in-
fluenced by the input u. These combinations evolve by themselves and are calledA-combinations.
On the other hand, category F is more specific than categoryA. It is a collection of submodels Fmn
which exhibit characteristic behavior of Frozen evolution. In these submodels, there exist m com-
binations of the plastic strain and the backstress which do not evolve at all and remain frozen during
elastoplastic deformation processes; hence, they are called F-combinations. The superscript m de-
notes the number of combinations which either are not influenced by the input (A-combinations)
or do not evolve (F-combinations) and the subscript n denotes the n-th submodel for a certain m.
Note that the internal state x of plastic strain and backstress and the state-space form (3) of
model (1) take the same forms for four cases of the model with linearly or nonlinearly volumetric

shown in Fig.1. On the basis of the classification, one could recognize that the material belongs to
which submodel of 9+Niso parameters model (1). While the correct submodel of material is known,
the parameter estimation will be implemented subsequently. Our method is a qualitative method
which is completely different from the method that directly estimates all parameters and then sets
some parameters, whose estimated values are close to zero, to be zero. In our method, we observed
the behavior of the material whose stress and strain information is recorded during the experiments.
Then, according to the information, we decided the material belongs to which submodel that some
parameters should be zero. The estimation of all parameters in the submodel is the last step. In the
following, we elaborate the whole identification procedure.
6.1 Estimation of isotropic hardening
The elementary step of identification procedure is the determination of elastic parameters Î¸1 and
Î¸2. Basing on the values of parameters Î¸1 and Î¸2, the detection of initial yield surface would be
carried out following. In next stage, the experiment is conducted with strain controlled path which
consists of cyclic loading in axial direction (11, 22, or 33) and shear direction (12, 13, or 23).
The response of stress Ïƒij will be recorded and then the plastic strain 
p
ij will be determined. In
each cycle, it consists of the four parts, loading, unloading, reverse loading, and reverse unloading.
Keeping the direction of plastic strain rate at the first yield point of reverse loading (t = t2) as the
opposite direction of plastic strain rate at the final point (t = t1) of loading region, we obtain the
following relations.
Qa(t1) = s(Î»(t1))n, Qa(t2) = âˆ’s(Î»(t2))n,
where s âˆˆ R denotes the Î»âˆ’valued scalar function and n âˆˆ R6 denotes the normal vector nTn = 1.
Assuming no difference between Î»(t1) and Î»(t2) i.e. Î»(t1) = Î»(t1) = Î»1, we derive
Q(t1) + Q(t2) = 2Qb(Î»1),
Q(t1)âˆ’Q(t2) = 2s(Î»1)n.
and
r(Î»1) =
1
8
(Q(t1)âˆ’Q(t2))TY(Q(t1)âˆ’Q(t2)). (6)
According to Eq.(6), one can observe the behavior of isotropic hardening. We will explain how to
use this equation in following section.
6.2 Determination of submodel
The cyclic loading experiment is conducted with strain controlled path which is proposed in last
section and the plastic strain is also recorded. If p33 âˆ’ p11, p22 âˆ’ p11, p23, p13, and p12 are invariant
22
After above-mentioned steps, the values of parameters for correct submodel will be estimated at
once. We summarized the identification procedure based on the classification as a flowchart shown
in Fig.18.
7 Conclusions
In this paper, we study the 9+Niso parameters model (Eq.(1)) and parted them into 18 submodels
which can be collected into two categories A and F . Eleven and 7 submodels in category A and
F , respectively. We obtainedAâˆ’ and Fâˆ’combinations for each submodel by using the state space
form (Eq.(3)) of equivalent vector form (Eq.(2)) and the linear combination matrices (Table 2). The
evolution properties of these combinations are pointed out, that are autonomic forAâˆ’combinations
and frozen forFâˆ’combinations, not only by the theoretical approach but also the numerical results.
Besides the non-reduced state mentioned above, we further considered a particular situation of
9+Niso parameters model (Eq.(1)) with linearly volumetric elasticity in axial-torsional-hoop exper-
iment under stress and mixed controlled. For both controlled cases, we explored its 15 submodels
and the corresponding evolution properties. Note that each submodel in the state of axial-torsional-
hoop experiment are relative to the submodels of non-reduced state.
Recognition of the behavior of each submodel helped us to construct the classification of 9+Niso
parameters model. The classification which is the same for the non-reduced state of both strain and
stress controlled cases with linearly or nonlinearly volumetric elasticity was organized in Table 2.
The similar results of the classification for axial-torsional-hoop experiment under stress and mixed
controlled are listed in Table 3. On the basis of the classification, we could identify the current
submodel of material.
We designed a identification procedure and summarized in Fig.18. The initial yield surface
detection is first step in this procedure. During the yield point detection, the elastic parameters Î¸1
and Î¸2 are estimated necessarily. In the next step, the experiment of cyclic loading is implemented.
The experimental data of cyclic loading enables us to observe the behavior of isotropic hardening
and to recognize the current submodel of material by using the criteria proposed in Section 5. The
last step of identification procedure is estimation of the parameters within the submodel.
This qualitative approach which addresses to capture characteristic behavior is different from
the kind of the curve fitting methods that directly estimates whole parameters of model. The con-
cept of classification is important for identification but also beneficial to modeling.
